2b:["$","p",null,{"className":"x:not-first:mt-[1.25em] x:leading-7","children":"But if you want to make AI output deterministic — parse it, validate it, and correct it in a loop until it converges — there is no alternative to structured output. Free-form text can’t be mechanically verified. Natural language can’t be compiled. Without structure, there’s no feedback loop, and without a feedback loop, there’s no guarantee. So we didn’t have the luxury of giving up on function calling. We had to make it work on the exact kind of complex, recursive schemas the industry had written off."}]

3d:["$","p",null,{"className":"x:not-first:mt-[1.25em] x:leading-7","children":["AutoBe uses ",["$","strong",null,{"children":"function calling"}],". 62:["$","p",null,{"className":"x:not-first:mt-[1.25em] x:leading-7","children":["This is the key point. The schema generated by the Typia compiler from source code types powers every runtime function that follows. The schema that ",["$","code",null,{"className":"nextra-code","dir":"ltr","children":"parse()"}]," references when recovering broken JSON and coercing types, the schema that ",["$","code",null,{"className":"nextra-code","dir":"ltr","children":"validate()"}]," uses as the comparison target when diagnosing errors — they’re all the same schema, automatically generated from types at compile time. 96:["$","p",null,{"className":"x:not-first:mt-[1.25em] x:leading-7","children":"If you’ve built a prompt-based AI agent, you’ve written prohibition rules:"}]
97:["$","ul",null,{"className":"x:[:is(ol,ul)_&]:my-[.75em] x:not-first:mt-[1.25em] x:list-disc x:ms-[1.5em]","children":["\n",["$","li",null,{"className":"x:my-[.5em]","children":"“Don’t create utility functions”"}],"\n",["$","li",null,{"className":"x:my-[.5em]","children":["“Don’t use the ",["$","code",null,{"className":"nextra-code","dir":"ltr","children":"any"}]," type”"]}],"\n",["$","li",null,{"className":"x:my-[.5em]","children":"“Don’t create circular dependencies”"}],"\n"]}]
98:["$","p",null,{"className":"x:not-first:mt-[1.25em] x:leading-7","children":"“Don’t think of a pink elephant.” The first thing that comes to mind is a pink elephant. When you tell an LLM “don’t do X,” X gets placed at the center of attention. To avoid a forbidden pattern, the model must first recall that pattern, which paradoxically increases its generation probability. This is the essence of token prediction."}]
99:["$","p",null,{"className":"x:not-first:mt-[1.25em] x:leading-7","children":"Even knowing this, you can’t avoid prohibition rules in prompts. “Don’t do X” is the only way natural language can express constraints."}]
9a:["$","p",null,{"className":"x:not-first:mt-[1.25em] x:leading-7","children":["$","strong",null,{"children":"With schemas, this problem disappears."}]}]
9b:["$","p",null,{"className":"x:not-first:mt-[1.25em] x:leading-7","children":["No need to say “don’t use the ",["$","code",null,{"className":"nextra-code","dir":"ltr","children":"any"}]," type” — if ",["$","code",null,{"className":"nextra-code","dir":"ltr","children":"any"}]," doesn’t exist in the schema, the LLM physically cannot generate it. No need to say “don’t create utility functions” — if there’s no slot for utility functions, that’s the end of it. When field types are limited to ",["$","code",null,{"className":"nextra-code","dir":"ltr","children":"\"boolean\" | \"int\" | \"double\" | \"string\" | \"uri\" | \"uuid\" | \"datetime\""}]," — 7 choices — there’s no path for the LLM to write ",["$","code",null,{"className":"nextra-code","dir":"ltr","children":"\"varchar\""}],"."]}]
9c:["$","p",null,{"className":"x:not-first:mt-[1.25em] x:leading-7","children":["Not prohibition, but ",["$","strong",null,{"children":"absence"}],". Prompts prohibit what you don’t want. Schemas allow only what you do want."]}]
